<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<p>The <b>Australian Broadcasting Corporation</b> (<b>ABC</b>) is <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>'s national <a href="/wiki/Public_broadcasting" title="Public broadcasting">public broadcaster</a>, owned and funded by the government. The ABC plays a leading role in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_broadcasting_in_Australia" title="History of broadcasting in Australia">history of broadcasting in Australia</a>. With a total annual budget of A$1.22 billion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-budgetreport_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-budgetreport-1">[1]</a></sup> the corporation provides television, radio, online and mobile services throughout metropolitan and regional Australia, as well as overseas through <a href="/wiki/Australia_Plus" title="Australia Plus">Australia Plus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radio_Australia" title="Radio Australia">Radio Australia</a>.</p>
<p>Founded in 1929 as the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Broadcasting_Company" title="Australian Broadcasting Company">Australian Broadcasting Company</a>, it was subsequently made a state-owned corporation on 1 July 1932 as the <b>Australian Broadcasting Commission</b>. The <i>Australian Broadcasting Corporation Act 1983</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-act83_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc-act83-2">[2]</a></sup> changed the name of the organisation to the Australian Broadcasting Corporation, effective 1 July 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-act83_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-abc-act83-2">[2]</a></sup> Although funded and owned by the government, the ABC remains <a href="/wiki/Editorial_independence" title="Editorial independence">editorially independent</a> as ensured through the <i>Australian Broadcasting Corporation Act 1983</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-act83_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-abc-act83-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ABC is sometimes informally referred to as "Aunty",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewTeethForAunty_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewTeethForAunty-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> originally in imitation of the <a href="/wiki/BBC#Attitudes_toward_the_BBC_in_popular_culture" title="BBC">British Broadcasting Corporation's nickname</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="History of the Australian Broadcasting Corporation">History of the Australian Broadcasting Corporation</a></div>
<h3><span id="1920s–40s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1920s.E2.80.9340s">1920s–40s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1920s–40s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Australian_Broadcasting_Company" title="Australian Broadcasting Company">Australian Broadcasting Company</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ABC_Mobile_Studio_Caravan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/ABC_Mobile_Studio_Caravan.jpg/220px-ABC_Mobile_Studio_Caravan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/ABC_Mobile_Studio_Caravan.jpg/330px-ABC_Mobile_Studio_Caravan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/ABC_Mobile_Studio_Caravan.jpg/440px-ABC_Mobile_Studio_Caravan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ABC_Mobile_Studio_Caravan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
ABC mobile studio <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Travel_trailer" title="Travel trailer">caravan</a>, used for concerts presented by the ABC at army camps and other locations, 1940</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first public radio station in Australia opened in Sydney on 23 November 1923 under the call sign 2SB with other stations in Melbourne, <a href="/wiki/Brisbane" title="Brisbane">Brisbane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hobart" title="Hobart">Hobart</a> following.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abcradio100_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-abcradio100-7">[7]</a></sup> A licensing scheme, administered by the <a href="/wiki/Postmaster-General%27s_Department" title="Postmaster-General's Department">Postmaster-General's Department</a>, was soon established allowing certain stations government funding, albeit with restrictions placed on their advertising content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langdon95_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-langdon95-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following a 1927 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_commission" title="Royal commission">royal commission</a> inquiry into radio licensing issues, the government established the National Broadcasting Service which subsequently took over a number of the larger funded stations. It also nationalised the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Broadcasting_Company" title="Australian Broadcasting Company">Australian Broadcasting Company</a> which had been created by entertainment interests to supply programs to various radio stations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langdon95_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-langdon95-8">[8]</a></sup> On 1 July 1932, the Australian Broadcasting Commission was established, taking over the operations of the National Broadcasting Service and eventually establishing offices in each of Australia's capital cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-langdon95_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-langdon95-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABC010712_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABC010712-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ABC_Perth.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="550" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/ABC_Perth.jpg/220px-ABC_Perth.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/ABC_Perth.jpg/330px-ABC_Perth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/ABC_Perth.jpg/440px-ABC_Perth.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ABC_Perth.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The ABC's <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a> headquarters in 1937</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Over the next four years the stations were reformed into a cohesive broadcasting organisation through regular program relays, coordinated by a centralised bureaucracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcasthistory_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-bcasthistory-10">[10]</a></sup> The Australian broadcast radio spectrum was constituted of the ABC and the commercial sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcasthistory_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-bcasthistory-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>News broadcasts were initially restricted, due to pressure from <a href="/wiki/Keith_Murdoch" title="Keith Murdoch">Sir Keith Murdoch</a>, who controlled many Australian newspapers. However, journalists such as Frank Dixon and <a href="/wiki/John_Hinde" title="John Hinde">John Hinde</a> began to subvert the agreements in the late 1930s. in 1939, Warren Denning was appointed to Canberra as the first ABC political correspondent, after Murdoch had refused to allow his newspapers to cover a speech by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lyons" title="Joseph Lyons">Joseph Lyons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1942 <i>The Australian Broadcasting Act</i> was passed, giving the ABC the power to decide when, and in what circumstances, political speeches should be broadcast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-waryears_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc-waryears-12">[12]</a></sup> Directions from the Minister about whether or not to broadcast any matter now had to be made in writing, and any exercise of the power had to be mentioned in the Commission's Annual Report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-waryears_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-abc-waryears-12">[12]</a></sup> It was used only once, in 1963.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-waryears_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-abc-waryears-12">[12]</a></sup> In the same year, "Kindergarten of the Air" began on ABC Radio in Perth, and was later broadcast nationally.</p>
<p>Cater argues that reform was urgently needed in 1945:</p>
<dl>
<dd>By the end of World War II, the ABC was a decadent, hollow institution. Its authority had been compromised by a poorly drafted charter and further undermined by timid management, poor governance and creeping wartime censorship. In April 1945, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Boyer_(broadcaster)" title="Richard Boyer (broadcaster)">Boyer</a> refused to accept the post of chairman until <a href="/wiki/John_Curtin" title="John Curtin">Prime Minister Curtin</a> issued a mandate of independence which Boyer drafted itself. The ABC under Boyer and general manager <a href="/wiki/Charles_Moses" title="Charles Moses">Charles Moses</a> invested as best it could in the cultural capital of the nation, establishing viable symphony orchestras and seizing on the potential of television.... [Boyer's] neutrality was never seriously questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span id="1950s–70s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1950s.E2.80.9370s">1950s–70s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1950s–70s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/ABC_Television" title="ABC Television">ABC Television</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:512px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv/512px--The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:512px;height:288px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="183.36798185941" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/71/The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 512 × 288 (1.05 Mbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="512" data-height="288" data-bandwidth="1048791" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/71/The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv/The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv.160p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="284" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="211760" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/71/The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv/The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="322456" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;track src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/index.php?title=TimedText:The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv.en.srt&amp;amp;action=raw&amp;amp;ctype=text%2Fx-srt" kind="subtitles" type="text/x-srt" srclang="en" label="English (en) subtitles" data-mwtitle="TimedText:The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv.en.srt" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-dir="ltr"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:The start of TV ABC.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv/220px--The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/71/The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_start_of_TV_ABC.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first broadcast of ABC TV – presented by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Charlton" title="Michael Charlton">Michael Charlton</a>, 5 November 1956</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jamesdibble.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="376" data-file-width="450" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ea/Jamesdibble.jpg/220px-Jamesdibble.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ea/Jamesdibble.jpg/330px-Jamesdibble.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ea/Jamesdibble.jpg/440px-Jamesdibble.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jamesdibble.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/James_Dibble" title="James Dibble">James Dibble</a>, reading the first <a href="/wiki/ABC_News_(Australia)" title="ABC News (Australia)">ABC News</a> television bulletin in NSW, 1956</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The ABC commenced television broadcasting in 1956, and followed the earlier radio practice of naming the station after the first letter of the base state. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABN-2" title="ABN-2">ABN-2</a> (New South Wales) Sydney was inaugurated by Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Robert_Menzies" title="Robert Menzies">Robert Menzies</a> on 5 November 1956, with the first broadcast presented by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Charlton" title="Michael Charlton">Michael Charlton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_Dibble" title="James Dibble">James Dibble</a> reading the first television news bulletin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABV-2" title="ABV-2">ABV-2</a> (Melbourne, Victoria) followed two weeks later, on 18 November 1956. Stations in other capital cities followed: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABQ-2" title="ABQ-2">ABQ-2</a> (Brisbane, Queensland) (1959), <a href="/wiki/ABS-2" title="ABS-2">ABS-2</a> (Adelaide, South Australia) (1960), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABW-2" title="ABW-2">ABW-2</a> (Perth, Western Australia) (1960), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABT-2" title="ABT-2">ABT-2</a> (Hobart, Tasmania) (1960). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABC-9" title="ABC-9">ABC-3</a> Canberra opened in 1961, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABD-6" title="ABD-6">ABD-6</a> (Darwin, Northern Territories) started broadcasting in 1971, both named after the base city.</p>
<p>Although radio programs could be distributed nationally by <a href="/wiki/Landline" title="Landline">landline</a>, television relay facilities were not in place until the early 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-50s_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc-50s-15">[15]</a></sup> This meant that news bulletins had to be sent to each capital city by <a href="/wiki/Teleprinter" title="Teleprinter">teleprinter</a>, to be prepared and presented separately in each city, with filmed materials copied manually and sent to each state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-50s_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-abc-50s-15">[15]</a></sup> Other television programs at the time included the popular <i><a href="/wiki/Six_O%27Clock_Rock" title="Six O'Clock Rock">Six O'Clock Rock</a></i> hosted by <a href="/wiki/Johnny_O%27Keefe" title="Johnny O'Keefe">Johnny O'Keefe</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mr._Squiggle" title="Mr. Squiggle">Mr. Squiggle</a></i>, as well as operas and plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-50s_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-abc-50s-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1973 <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales_Rugby_League" title="New South Wales Rugby League">New South Wales Rugby League</a> boss <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Humphreys_(rugby_league)" title="Kevin Humphreys (rugby league)">Kevin Humphreys</a> negotiated rugby league's first television deal with the ABC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> In 1975, colour television was introduced into Australia, and within a decade the ABC had moved into satellite broadcasting, greatly enhancing its ability to distribute content nationally. In the same year, the ABC introduced a 24-hour-a-day AM rock station in Sydney, 2JJ (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_Jay_(radio_network)" title="Double Jay (radio network)">Double Jay</a>), which was eventually expanded into the national <a href="/wiki/Triple_J" title="Triple J">Triple J</a> FM network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-60s70s_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc-60s70s-17">[17]</a></sup> A year later, a national classical music network was established on the <a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulation" title="Frequency modulation">FM</a> band, broadcasting from Adelaide. It was initially known as ABC-FM (now called <a href="/wiki/ABC_Classic_FM" title="ABC Classic FM">ABC Classic FM</a>) – referring both to its 'fine music' programming and radio frequency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-60s70s_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-abc-60s70s-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>ABC budget cuts began in 1976 and continued until 1985. In 1978 the ABC NSW Staff Association organised a strike against budget cuts and political interference. Sydney ABC was off air for four days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> A packed free concert in support was held at the <a href="/wiki/Regent_Theatre_(Sydney)" title="Regent Theatre (Sydney)">Regent Theatre</a> and compered by <a href="/wiki/Bob_Hudson_(singer)" title="Bob Hudson (singer)">Bob Hudson</a>. It featured <a href="/wiki/Fred_Dagg" title="Fred Dagg">Fred Dagg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robyn_Archer" title="Robyn Archer">Robyn Archer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In 1991, Tom Molomby wrote:</p>
<dl>
<dd>"The effects of the budget reductions had been so badly handled that the organisation was to remain seriously crippled for years."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span id="1980s–90s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s.E2.80.9390s">1980s–90s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1980s–90s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>Australian Broadcasting Corporation Act 1983</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-act83_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-abc-act83-2">[2]</a></sup> changed the name of the organisation from the "Australian Broadcasting Commission" to the "Australian Broadcasting Corporation", effective 1 July 1983.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-act83_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-abc-act83-2">[2]</a></sup> At the same time, the newly formed Corporation underwent significant restructuring. The ABC was split into separate television and radio divisions, with an overhaul of management, finance, property and engineering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-80s_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc-80s-21">[21]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Geoffrey_Whitehead_(communications)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Geoffrey Whitehead (communications) (page does not exist)">Geoffrey Whitehead</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> was the initial managing director; however, following his resignation in 1986, <a href="/wiki/David_Hill_(businessman)" title="David Hill (businessman)">David Hill</a> (at the time chair of the ABC Board) took over his position.</p>
<p>Program production in <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australians" title="Indigenous Australians">indigenous</a> affairs, comedy, social history and current affairs was significantly expanded, while the Corporation's output of drama was boosted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-80s_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-abc-80s-21">[21]</a></sup> Local production trebled from 1986–91 with the assistance of co-production, co-financing, and pre-sales arrangements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-80s_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-abc-80s-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>A new Concert Music Department was formed in 1985 to co-ordinate the corporation's six symphony orchestras, which in turn received a greater level of autonomy to better respond to local needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-80s_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-abc-80s-21">[21]</a></sup> Open-air free concerts and tours, educational activities, and joint ventures with other music groups were undertaken at the time to expand the orchestras' audience reach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-80s_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-abc-80s-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>ABC Radio was restructured significantly again in 1985 – Radio One became the Metropolitan network, while Radio 2 became known as <a href="/wiki/Radio_National" title="Radio National">Radio National</a> (callsigns, however, were not standardised until 1990). New programs such as <i>The World Today</i>, <i>Australia All Over</i>, and <i>The Coodabeen Champions</i> were introduced, while <a href="/wiki/ABC_Classic_FM" title="ABC Classic FM">ABC-FM</a> established an Australian Music Unit in 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-80s_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-abc-80s-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radio_Australia" title="Radio Australia">Radio Australia</a> began to focus on the Asia-Pacific region, with coverage targeted at the south west and central Pacific, south-east Asia, and north Asia. Radio Australia also carried more news coverage, with special broadcasts during the <a href="/wiki/1987_Fijian_coups_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1987 Fijian coups d'état">1987 Fijian coup</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiananmen_Square_massacre" title="Tiananmen Square massacre">Tiananmen Square massacre</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Gulf_War" title="First Gulf War">First Gulf War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-80s_21-6"><a href="#cite_note-abc-80s-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ABC_Sydney.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="336" data-file-width="448" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/ABC_Sydney.jpg/220px-ABC_Sydney.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/ABC_Sydney.jpg/330px-ABC_Sydney.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/ABC_Sydney.jpg/440px-ABC_Sydney.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ABC_Sydney.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The ABC's Sydney headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Ultimo,_New_South_Wales" title="Ultimo, New South Wales">Ultimo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1991, the Corporation's Sydney radio and orchestral operations moved to a new building built by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leighton_Holdings" title="Leighton Holdings">Leighton Holdings</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> on a single site in the inner-city suburb of <a href="/wiki/Ultimo,_New_South_Wales" title="Ultimo, New South Wales">Ultimo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-90s_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc-90s-24">[24]</a></sup> In Melbourne, the ABC Southbank Centre was completed in 1994, and now houses the radio division in Victoria as well as the Melbourne Symphony Orchestra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-90s_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-abc-90s-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ABC Multimedia Unit was established in July 1995, to manage the new ABC website (launched in August). Funding was allocated later that year specifically for online content, as opposed to reliance on funding for television and radio content. The first online election coverage was put together in 1996, and included news, electorate maps, candidate information and live results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-90s_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-abc-90s-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the early 1990s, all major ABC broadcasting outlets moved to 24-hour-a-day operation, while regional radio coverage in Australia was extended with 80 new transmitters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-90s_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-abc-90s-24">[24]</a></sup> Live television broadcasts of selected parliamentary sessions started in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-90s_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-abc-90s-24">[24]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABC_NewsRadio" title="ABC NewsRadio">ABC NewsRadio</a>, a continuous news network broadcast on the <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_and_News_Network" title="Parliamentary and News Network">Parliamentary and News Network</a> when parliament is not sitting, was launched on 5 October 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-90s_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-abc-90s-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>International television service <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australia_Television_International" title="Australia Television International">Australia Television International</a> was established in 1993, while at the same time Radio Australia increased its international reach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-90s_24-6"><a href="#cite_note-abc-90s-24">[24]</a></sup> Reduced funding in 1997 for Radio Australia resulted in staff and programming cuts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-90s_24-7"><a href="#cite_note-abc-90s-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Australia Television</i> was sold to the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Network" title="Seven Network">Seven Network</a> in 1998, however the service continued to show <a href="/wiki/ABC_News_and_Current_Affairs" title="ABC News and Current Affairs">ABC news and current affairs programming</a> up until its closure in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mr_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-mr-25">[25]</a></sup> The ABC's television operation joined its radio and online divisions at the corporation's Ultimo headquarters in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-00s_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc-00s-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2000s">2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2001, digital television commenced after four years of preparation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-00s_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-abc-00s-26">[26]</a></sup> In readiness, the ABC had fully digitised its production, post-production and transmission facilities – heralded at the time as "the greatest advance in television technology since the introduction of colour".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-00s_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-abc-00s-26">[26]</a></sup> The first programmes to be produced in widescreen were drama series <i><a href="/wiki/Something_in_the_Air_(TV_series)" title="Something in the Air (TV series)">Something in the Air</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Grass_Roots_(TV_series)" title="Grass Roots (TV series)">Grass Roots</a></i> and <i>In the Mind of the Architect</i>.</p>
<p>At the same time, the ABC's Multimedia division was renamed "ABC New Media", becoming an output division of the ABC alongside Television and Radio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-00s_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-abc-00s-26">[26]</a></sup> Legislation allowed the ABC to provide 'multichannels' – additional, digital-only, television services managed by the New Media Division. Soon after the introduction of digital television in 2001, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fly_TV" title="Fly TV">Fly TV</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABC_Kids_channel" title="ABC Kids channel">ABC Kids channel</a> launched, showing a mix of programming aimed at teenagers and children.</p>
<p>In 2002, the ABC launched <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australia_Network" title="Australia Network">ABC Asia Pacific</a> – the replacement for the defunct Australia Television International operated previously by the Seven Network. Much like its predecessor, and companion radio network <a href="/wiki/Radio_Australia" title="Radio Australia">Radio Australia</a>, the service provided a mix of programming targeted at audiences throughout the Asia-Pacific region. Funding cuts in 2003 led to the closure of Fly TV and the ABC Kids channel.</p>
<p>The ABC launched a digital radio service, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABC_DiG" title="ABC DiG">ABC DiG</a>, in November 2002, available though the internet and digital television, but not available through any other terrestrial broadcast until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DAB%2B" title="DAB+">DAB+</a> became available in 2009.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abc2promo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="191" data-file-width="340" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d3/Abc2promo.jpg/220px-Abc2promo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d3/Abc2promo.jpg/330px-Abc2promo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d3/Abc2promo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abc2promo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/ABC2" title="ABC2">ABC2</a> launched on 7 March 2005</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/ABC2" title="ABC2">ABC2</a>, a second attempt at a digital-only television channel, launched on 7 March 2005. Unlike its predecessors the new service was not dependent on government funding, instead running on a budget of A$3 million per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whoseabc_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-whoseabc-27">[27]</a></sup> Minister for Communications <a href="/wiki/Helen_Coonan" title="Helen Coonan">Helen Coonan</a> inaugurated the channel at <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Australia" title="Parliament of Australia">Parliament House</a> three days later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Genre restrictions limiting the types of programming the channel could carry were lifted in October 2006 – ABC2 was henceforth able to carry programming classified as comedy, drama, national news, sport and entertainment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>A high incidence of breast cancer in female staff working at the ABC's offices in Brisbane led to the closure of the site, based in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toowong,_Queensland" title="Toowong, Queensland">Toowong</a>, on 21 December 2006. Sixteen women were diagnosed with the disease in a period spanning 1994 to 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aus-breastcancer_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-aus-breastcancer-30">[30]</a></sup> A progress report released in March 2007 by an independent panel formed to investigate the occurrences found that the rate of occurrence for breast cancer rate at the offices was eleven times higher than elsewhere<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aus-furthertests_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-aus-furthertests-31">[31]</a></sup> – after the closure of the site, the ABC's Brisbane-based television and radio operations were moved to alternate locations around the city, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TVQ-10" title="TVQ-10">Ten Brisbane</a>'s studios at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mt_Coot-tha" title="Mt Coot-tha">Mt Coot-tha</a>. The ABC's managing director, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Scott_(businessman)" title="Mark Scott (businessman)">Mark Scott</a>, announced in August 2007 that new studios would be built on the site, following the final release of the Review and Scientific Investigation Panel's report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brisbanestudiosrelease_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-brisbanestudiosrelease-32">[32]</a></sup> In January 2012 the ABC in Brisbane moved into purpose-built accommodation in <a href="/wiki/South_Bank,_Queensland" title="South Bank, Queensland">South Bank</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 8 February 2008, ABC TV was rebranded as <a href="/wiki/ABC_(Australian_TV_channel)" title="ABC (Australian TV channel)">ABC1</a>, complementing the existing <a href="/wiki/ABC2" title="ABC2">ABC2</a> digital-only channel which was launched on 7 March 2005. Branding was also added for a new kids' channel that had been announced throughout the Howard Government based on their winning the 2007 election but left to the 2009 Rudd Government Budget where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABC3" title="ABC3">ABC3</a> was funded and announced in June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rebrand_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-rebrand-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> A new online video-on-demand service launched in July of the same year, titled <a href="/wiki/ABC_iview" title="ABC iview">ABC iview</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-announce_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-announce-36">[36]</a></sup> and the ABC launched <a href="/wiki/Digital_radio_in_Australia" title="Digital radio in Australia">digital radio</a> broadcasts in the same month.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010s">2010s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2010s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABC_News_24" title="ABC News 24">ABC News 24</a> launched on 22 July 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and brought with it both new programming content as well as a collaboration of existing news and current affair productions and resources. The ABC launched the 24-hour news channel to both complement its existing 24-hour <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABC_NewsRadio" title="ABC NewsRadio">ABC News Radio</a> service and compete with commercial offerings on cable TV. It became the ABC's fifth domestic TV channel and the fourth launched within the past 10 years.</p>
<p>On 20 July 2014, <a href="/wiki/ABC_(Australian_TV_channel)" title="ABC (Australian TV channel)">ABC1</a> was renamed back to its original name of ABC.</p>
<p>In 2014 the ABC ran its first "Mental As" week focusing on improving awareness of mental health issues, as part of <a href="/wiki/Mental_Health_Week_(Australia)" title="Mental Health Week (Australia)">Mental Health Week</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2015 it was announced that former Google executive <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Guthrie" title="Michelle Guthrie">Michelle Guthrie</a> would take over from managing director Mark Scott, who was to retire in April 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abcnews_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-abcnews-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporation">Corporation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Corporation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Australian_Broadcasting_Corporation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/ABC_Board" title="ABC Board">ABC Board</a></div>
<p>The operations of the ABC are governed by a board of directors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> consisting of a managing director,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boardmembership_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-boardmembership-42">[42]</a></sup> five to seven directors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boardmembership_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-boardmembership-42">[42]</a></sup> and until 2006, a staff-elected director.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boardmembership_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-boardmembership-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-restructure_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-restructure-43">[43]</a></sup> The managing director is appointed by the board for a period of up to five years, but is eligible for renewal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The authority and guidelines for the appointment of directors is provided for in the <i>Australian Broadcasting Corporation Act 1983</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-act83_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-abc-act83-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abouttheboard_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abouttheboard-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Board_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Board-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Appointments to the <a href="/wiki/ABC_Board" title="ABC Board">ABC Board</a> made by successive governments have often resulted in criticism of the appointees' political affiliation, background, and relative merit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aboveboard_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-aboveboard-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pm-politics_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-pm-politics-48">[48]</a></sup> Past appointments have associated directly with political parties – five of fourteen appointed chairmen have been accused of political affiliation or friendship, include Richard Downing and <a href="/wiki/Ken_Myer" title="Ken Myer">Ken Myer</a> (both of whom publicly endorsed the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Labor_Party" title="Australian Labor Party">Australian Labor Party</a> at the 1972 election),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whoseabc_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-whoseabc-27">[27]</a></sup> as well as Sir Henry Bland. <a href="/wiki/David_Hill_(businessman)" title="David Hill (businessman)">David Hill</a> was close to <a href="/wiki/Neville_Wran" title="Neville Wran">Neville Wran</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Donald_McDonald_(ABC_chairperson)" title="Donald McDonald (ABC chairperson)">Donald McDonald</a> was considered to be a close friend of <a href="/wiki/John_Howard" title="John Howard">John Howard</a>.</p>
<p>From 2003 the <a href="/wiki/Howard_Government" title="Howard Government">Howard Government</a> made several controversial appointments to the ABC Board, including prominent ABC critic <a href="/wiki/Janet_Albrechtsen" title="Janet Albrechtsen">Janet Albrechtsen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pm-albrechtsen_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-pm-albrechtsen-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ron_Brunton" title="Ron Brunton">Ron Brunton</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Keith_Windschuttle" title="Keith Windschuttle">Keith Windschuttle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pm-politics_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-pm-politics-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>During their <a href="/wiki/Australian_federal_election,_2007" title="Australian federal election, 2007">2007 federal election campaign</a>, Labor announced plans to introduce a new system, similar to that of the BBC, for appointing members to the board.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alpboardappt_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-alpboardappt-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newapp_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-newapp-53">[53]</a></sup> Under the new system, candidates for the ABC Board would be considered by an independent panel established "at arm's length" from the Communications Minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> If the Minister chose someone not on the panel's shortlist, they would be required to justify this to parliament. The ABC chairman would be nominated by the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Australia" title="Prime Minister of Australia">Prime Minister</a> and endorsed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(Australia)" title="Leader of the Opposition (Australia)">Leader of the Opposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alpboardappt_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-alpboardappt-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loyalists_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-loyalists-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-markday_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-markday-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The new merit-based appointment system was announced on 16 October, in advance of the new triennial funding period starting in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mb-board_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-mb-board-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mb-boardrules_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-mb-boardrules-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Current Board members are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boardlist_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-boardlist-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
